class="firstHeading" lang="en">Robert Burns</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other people named class="mw-empty-elt">\n\n</p>\n<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold"><span class="fn">Robert Sherramuir</a>"</li></ul>\n</div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;">\n<hr /></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-top:0.225em;line-height:1.1em;padding-right:0.65em;">Signature</th><td style="line-height:1.4em;">\n<a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Burns_Signature.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Robert_Burns_Signature.svg/160px-Robert_Burns_Signature.svg.png" width="160" height="56" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Robert_Burns_Signature.svg/240px-Robert_Burns_Signature.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Robert_Burns_Signature.svg/320px-Robert_Burns_Signature.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="638" data-file-height="224" /></a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Robert Burns</b> (25 January 1759&#160;&#8211;&#32;21 July 1796), also known as <b>Rabbie Burns</b>, the <b>Bard of Ayrshire</b>, <b>Ploughman Poet</b> and various other names and <a href="/wiki/Epithet" title="Epithet">epithets</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup> was a Scottish poet and lyricist. He is widely regarded as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_poets" class="mw-redirect" title="List of national poets">national poet</a> of Scotland and is celebrated worldwide. He is the best known of the poets who have written in the <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots language</a>, although much of his writing is also in English and a light <a href="/wiki/Central_Scots" title="Central Scots">Scots dialect</a>, accessible to an audience beyond Scotland. He also wrote in standard English, and in these writings his political or civil commentary is often at its bluntest.\n</p><p>He is regarded as a pioneer of the <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic movement</a>, and after his death he became a great source of inspiration to the founders of both <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a>, and a cultural icon in Scotland and among the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_diaspora" title="Scottish diaspora">Scottish diaspora</a> around the world. Celebration of his life and work became almost a national <a href="/wiki/Charismatic_cult" class="mw-redirect" title="Charismatic cult">charismatic cult</a> during the 19th and 20th centuries, and his influence has long been strong on <a href="/wiki/Scottish_literature" title="Scottish literature">Scottish literature</a>. In 2009 he was chosen as the greatest Scot by the Scottish public in a vote run by Scottish television channel <a href="/wiki/STV_(TV_network)" class="mw-redirect" title="STV (TV network)">STV</a>.\n</p><p>As well as making original compositions, Burns also collected <a href="/wiki/Folk_music" title="Folk music">folk songs</a> from across Scotland, often revising or <a href="/wiki/Literary_adaptation" title="Literary adaptation">adapting</a> them. His poem (and song) "<a href="/wiki/Auld_Lang_Syne" title="Auld Lang Syne">Auld Lang Syne</a>" is often sung at <a href="/wiki/Hogmanay" title="Hogmanay">Hogmanay</a> (the last day of the year), and "<a href="/wiki/Scots_Wha_Hae" title="Scots Wha Hae">Scots Wha Hae</a>" served for a long time as an unofficial <a href="/wiki/National_anthem" title="National anthem">national anthem</a> of the country. Other poems and songs of Burns that remain well known across the world today include "<a href="/wiki/A_Red,_Red_Rose" title="A Red, Red Rose">A Red, Red Rose</a>", "<a href="/wiki/A_Man%27s_a_Man_for_A%27_That" class="mw-redirect" title="A Man&#39;s a Man for A&#39; That">A Man\'s a Man for A\' That</a>", "<a href="/wiki/To_a_Louse" title="To a Louse">To a Louse</a>", "<a href="/wiki/To_a_Mouse" title="To a Mouse">To a Mouse</a>", "<a href="/wiki/The_Battle_of_Sherramuir" title="The Battle of Sherramuir">The Battle of Sherramuir</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Tam_o%27_Shanter_(poem)" title="Tam o&#39; Shanter (poem)">Tam o\' Shanter</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Ae_Fond_Kiss" title="Ae Fond Kiss">Ae Fond Kiss</a>".\n</p>\n<div id="toc" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Geburtshaus_RobertBurns_3_10x15.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Geburtshaus_RobertBurns_3_10x15.jpg/220px-Geburtshaus_RobertBurns_3_10x15.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Geburtshaus_RobertBurns_3_10x15.jpg/330px-Geburtshaus_RobertBurns_3_10x15.jpg 1.5x, class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Geburtshaus_RobertBurns_3_10x15.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Burns Cottage in Alloway, Ayrshire</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_burns_family.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/The_burns_family.JPG/220px-The_burns_family.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/The_burns_family.JPG/330px-The_burns_family.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/The_burns_family.JPG/440px-The_burns_family.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2592" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_burns_family.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Inside the Burns Cottage</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alloway">Alloway</span></h3>\n<p>Burns was born two miles (3&#160;km) south of <a href="/wiki/Ayr" title="Ayr">Ayr</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Alloway" title="Alloway">Alloway</a>, the eldest of the seven children of <a href="/wiki/William_Burnes" title="William Burnes">William Burnes</a> (1721\xe2\x80\x931784), a self-educated tenant farmer from <a href="/wiki/Dunnottar" class="mw-redirect" title="Dunnottar">Dunnottar</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Kincardineshire" title="Kincardineshire">Mearns</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Agnes_Broun" title="Agnes Broun">Agnes Broun</a> (1732\xe2\x80\x931820), the daughter of a <a href="/wiki/Kirkoswald,_South_Ayrshire" title="Kirkoswald, South Ayrshire">Kirkoswald</a> tenant farmer.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>He was born in a house built by his father (now the <a href="/wiki/Burns_Cottage" title="Burns Cottage">Burns Cottage</a> Museum), where he lived until <a href="/wiki/Easter" title="Easter">Easter</a> 1766, when he was seven years old. William Burnes sold the house and took the tenancy of the 70-acre (280,000&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) Mount Oliphant farm, southeast of Alloway. Here Burns grew up in poverty and hardship, and the severe manual labour of the farm left its traces in a premature stoop and a weakened constitution.\n</p><p>He had little regular schooling and got much of his education from his father, who taught his children reading, writing, arithmetic, geography, and history and also wrote for them <i>A Manual Of Christian Belief</i>. He was also taught by John Murdoch (1747\xe2\x80\x931824), who opened an "<a href="/wiki/History_of_education_in_Scotland#adventure_schools" title="History of education in Scotland">adventure school</a>" in Alloway in 1763 and taught Latin, French, and mathematics to both Robert and his brother <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Burns_(Farmer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gilbert Burns (Farmer)">Gilbert</a> (1760\xe2\x80\x931827) from 1765 to 1768 until Murdoch left the parish. After a few years of home education, Burns was sent to Dalrymple Parish School in mid-1772 before returning at harvest time to full-time farm labouring until 1773, when he was sent to lodge with Murdoch for three weeks to study grammar, French, and Latin.\n</p><p>By the age of 15, Burns was the principal labourer at Mount Oliphant. During the harvest of 1774, he was assisted by <a href="/wiki/Nelly_Kilpatrick" title="Nelly Kilpatrick">Nelly Kilpatrick</a> (1759\xe2\x80\x931820), who inspired his first attempt at poetry, "O, Once I Lov\'d A Bonnie Lass". In 1775, he was sent to finish his education with a tutor at Kirkoswald, where he met <a href="/wiki/Peggy_Thompson" title="Peggy Thompson">Peggy Thompson</a> (born 1762), to whom he wrote two songs, "Now Westlin\' Winds" and "I Dream\'d I Lay".\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tarbolton">Tarbolton</span></h3>\n<p>Despite his ability and character, <a href="/wiki/William_Burnes" title="William Burnes">William Burnes</a> was consistently unfortunate, and migrated with his large family from farm to farm without ever being able to improve his circumstances. At <a href="/wiki/Whitsun" title="Whitsun">Whitsun</a>, 1777, he removed his large family from the unfavourable conditions of Mount Oliphant to the 130-acre (0.53&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) farm at Lochlea, near <a href="/wiki/Tarbolton" title="Tarbolton">Tarbolton</a>, where they stayed until William Burnes\'s death in 1784. Subsequently, the family became integrated into the community of Tarbolton. To his father\'s disapproval, Robert joined a country dancing school in 1779 and, with Gilbert, formed the <a href="/wiki/Bachelors%27_Club,_Tarbolton" title="Bachelors&#39; Club, Tarbolton">Tarbolton Bachelors\' Club</a> the following year. His earliest existing letters date from this time, when he began making romantic overtures to <a href="/wiki/Alison_Begbie" title="Alison Begbie">Alison Begbie</a> (b. 1762). In spite of four songs written for her and a suggestion that he was willing to marry her, she rejected him.\n</p><p>Robert Burns was <a href="/wiki/Entered_Apprentice" class="mw-redirect" title="Entered Apprentice">initiated</a> into masonic <a href="/wiki/Masonic_Lodge" class="mw-redirect" title="Masonic Lodge">Lodge</a> St David, Tarbolton, on 4 July 1781, when he was 22.\n</p><p>In December 1781, Burns moved temporarily to <a href="/wiki/Irvine,_Ayrshire" class="mw-redirect" title="Irvine, Ayrshire">Irvine</a> to learn to become a <a href="/wiki/Flax" title="Flax">flax</a>-dresser, but during the workers\' celebrations for <a href="/wiki/New_Year" title="New Year">New Year</a> 1781/1782 (which included Burns as a participant) the flax shop caught fire and was burnt to the ground. This venture accordingly came to an end, and Burns went home to Lochlea farm. During this time he met and befriended <a href="/wiki/Richard_Brown_(Captain)" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard Brown (Captain)">Captain Richard Brown</a> who encouraged him to become a poet.\n</p><p>He continued to write poems and songs and began a <a href="/wiki/Commonplace_book" title="Commonplace book">commonplace book</a> in 1783, while his father fought a legal dispute with his landlord. The case went to the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Session" title="Court of Session">Court of Session</a>, and Burnes was upheld in January 1784, a fortnight before he died.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mauchline">Mauchline</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PG_1063Burns_Naysmith.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/PG_1063Burns_Naysmith.jpg/170px-PG_1063Burns_Naysmith.jpg" width="170" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/PG_1063Burns_Naysmith.jpg/255px-PG_1063Burns_Naysmith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/PG_1063Burns_Naysmith.jpg/340px-PG_1063Burns_Naysmith.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5333" data-file-height="6556" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PG_1063Burns_Naysmith.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Full view of the Nasmyth portrait of 1787, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Portrait_Gallery" title="Scottish National Portrait Gallery">Scottish National Portrait Gallery</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Robert and Gilbert made an ineffectual struggle to keep on the farm, but after its failure they moved to the farm at Mossgiel, near <a href="/wiki/Mauchline" title="Mauchline">Mauchline</a>, in March, which they maintained with an uphill fight for the next four years. In mid-1784 Burns came to know a group of girls known collectively as The Belles of Mauchline, one of whom was <a href="/wiki/Jean_Armour" title="Jean Armour">Jean Armour</a>, the daughter of a <a href="/wiki/Stonemason" class="mw-redirect" title="Stonemason">stonemason</a> from Mauchline.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Love_affairs">Love affairs</span></h3>\n<p>His first child, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Bishop_(Burns)" title="Elizabeth Bishop (Burns)">Elizabeth "Bess" Burns</a> (1785\xe2\x80\x931817), was born to his mother\'s servant, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Paton" title="Elizabeth Paton">Elizabeth Paton</a> (1760\xe2\x80\x93circa 1799), while he was embarking on a relationship with Jean Armour, who became pregnant with twins in March 1786. Burns signed a paper attesting his marriage to Jean, but her father "was in the greatest distress, and fainted away". To avoid disgrace, her parents sent her to live with her uncle in <a href="/wiki/Paisley,_Renfrewshire" title="Paisley, Renfrewshire">Paisley</a>. Although Armour\'s father initially forbade it, they were eventually married in 1788.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Armour bore him nine children, only three of whom survived infancy.\n</p><p>Burns was in financial difficulties due to his want of success in farming, and to make enough money to support a family he took up an offer of work in Jamaica from Dr Patrick Douglas of Garrallan, <a href="/wiki/Old_Cumnock" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Cumnock">Old Cumnock</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/Sugar_plantations_in_the_Caribbean" title="Sugar plantations in the Caribbean">sugar plantations</a> outside <a href="/wiki/Port_Antonio" title="Port Antonio">Port Antonio</a> were managed by his brother Charles, under whom Burns was to be a "book keeper" (assistant overseer of slaves).<sup id="cite_ref-Crawford2011_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Crawford2011-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> It has been suggested that that was a position for a single man, and that he would live in rustic conditions, not likely to be living in the great house at a salary of <a href="/wiki/Pound_sterling" title="Pound sterling">\xc2\xa3</a>30 per annum.<sup id="cite_ref-bold19_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bold19-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HighlandMary_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HighlandMary-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>  Burns\'s egalitarian views were typified by "<a href="/wiki/The_Slave%27s_Lament" title="The Slave&#39;s Lament">The Slave\'s Lament</a>" six years later, but in 1786 there was little public awareness of the <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Abolitionism in the United Kingdom">abolitionist</a> movement that began about that time.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At about the same time, Burns fell in love with <a href="/wiki/Mary_Campbell_(Highland_Mary)" title="Mary Campbell (Highland Mary)">Mary Campbell</a> (1763\xe2\x80\x931786), whom he had seen in church while he was still living in Tarbolton. She was born near <a href="/wiki/Dunoon" title="Dunoon">Dunoon</a> and had lived in <a href="/wiki/Campbeltown" title="Campbeltown">Campbeltown</a> before moving to work in Ayrshire. He dedicated the poems "The Highland Lassie O", "Highland Mary", and "To Mary in Heaven" to her. His song "Will ye go to the Indies, my Mary, And leave auld Scotia\'s shore?" suggests that they planned to emigrate to Jamaica together. Their relationship has been the subject of much conjecture, and it has been suggested that on 14 May 1786 they exchanged Bibles and <a href="/wiki/Oath" title="Oath">plighted</a> their troth over the <a href="/wiki/Water_of_Fail" title="Water of Fail">Water of Fail</a> in a traditional form of marriage. Soon afterwards Mary Campbell left her work in Ayrshire, went to the seaport of <a href="/wiki/Greenock" title="Greenock">Greenock</a>, and sailed home to her parents in Campbeltown.<sup id="cite_ref-bold19_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bold19-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HighlandMary_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HighlandMary-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In October 1786, Mary and her father sailed from Campbeltown to visit her brother in Greenock. Her brother fell ill with <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a>, which she also caught while nursing him. She died of typhus on 20 or 21 October 1786 and <a href="/wiki/Greenock#Notable_residents" title="Greenock">was buried there</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HighlandMary_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HighlandMary-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kilmarnock_volume">Kilmarnock volume</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kilmarnockvolume.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b0/Kilmarnockvolume.jpg/220px-Kilmarnockvolume.jpg" width="220" height="340" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/b0/Kilmarnockvolume.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="254" data-file-height="392" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kilmarnockvolume.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Title page of the Kilmarnock Edition</div></div></div>\n<p>As Burns lacked the funds to pay for his passage to the West Indies, Gavin Hamilton suggested that he should "publish his poems in the mean time by subscription, as a likely way of getting a little money to provide him more liberally in necessaries for Jamaica." On 3 April Burns sent proposals for publishing his <i>Scotch Poems</i> to John Wilson, a local printer in <a href="/wiki/Kilmarnock" title="Kilmarnock">Kilmarnock</a>, who published these proposals on 14 April 1786, on the same day that Jean Armour\'s father tore up the paper in which Burns attested his marriage to Jean. To obtain a certificate that he was a free bachelor, Burns agreed on 25 June to stand for <a href="/wiki/Rebuke" title="Rebuke">rebuke</a> in the Mauchline kirk for three Sundays. He transferred his share in Mossgiel farm to his brother Gilbert on 22 July, and on 30 July wrote to tell his friend John Richmond that, "Armour has got a warrant to throw me in jail until I can find a warrant for an enormous sum&#160;... I am wandering from one friend\'s house to another."<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 31 July 1786 John Wilson published the volume of works by Robert Burns, <i>Poems, Chiefly in the Scottish dialect</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-bold20_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bold20-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Known as the <a href="/wiki/Kilmarnock_volume" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilmarnock volume">Kilmarnock volume</a>, it sold for 3 shillings and contained much of his best writing, including "The Twa Dogs", "<a href="/wiki/Address_to_the_Deil" title="Address to the Deil">Address to the Deil</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Halloween_(poem)" title="Halloween (poem)">Halloween</a>", "The Cotter\'s Saturday Night", "<a href="/wiki/To_a_Mouse" title="To a Mouse">To a Mouse</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Epitaph_for_James_Smith" title="Epitaph for James Smith">Epitaph for James Smith</a>", and "<a href="/wiki/To_a_Mountain_Daisy" title="To a Mountain Daisy">To a Mountain Daisy</a>", many of which had been written at Mossgiel farm. The success of the work was immediate, and soon he was known across the country.\n</p><p>Burns postponed his planned emigration to Jamaica on 1 September, and was at Mossgiel two days later when he learnt that Jean Armour had given birth to twins. On 4 September <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Blacklock" title="Thomas Blacklock">Thomas Blacklock</a> wrote a letter expressing admiration for the poetry in the Kilmarnock volume, and suggesting an enlarged second edition.<sup id="cite_ref-bold20_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bold20-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> A copy of it was passed to Burns, who later recalled, "I had taken the last farewell of my few friends, my chest was on the road to Greenock; I had composed the last song I should ever measure in Scotland&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 \'The Gloomy night is gathering fast\'&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 when a letter from Dr Blacklock to a friend of mine overthrew all my schemes, by opening new prospects to my poetic ambition. The Doctor belonged to a set of critics for whose applause I had not dared to hope. His opinion that I would meet with encouragement in Edinburgh for a second edition, fired me so much, that away I posted for that city, without a single acquaintance, or a single letter of introduction."<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Nasmyth_-_Robert_Burns,_1759_-_1796._Poet_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Alexander_Nasmyth_-_Robert_Burns%2C_1759_-_1796._Poet_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/170px-Alexander_Nasmyth_-_Robert_Burns%2C_1759_-_1796._Poet_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="170" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Alexander_Nasmyth_-_Robert_Burns%2C_1759_-_1796._Poet_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/255px-Alexander_Nasmyth_-_Robert_Burns%2C_1759_-_1796._Poet_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Alexander_Nasmyth_-_Robert_Burns%2C_1759_-_1796._Poet_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/340px-Alexander_Nasmyth_-_Robert_Burns%2C_1759_-_1796._Poet_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2914" data-file-height="4001" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Nasmyth_-_Robert_Burns,_1759_-_1796._Poet_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Nasmyth" title="Alexander Nasmyth">Alexander Nasmyth</a>, Robert Burns (1828).</div></div></div>\n<p>On 27 November 1786 Burns borrowed a pony and set out for <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>. On 14 December William Creech issued subscription bills for the first Edinburgh edition of <i>Poems, Chiefly in the Scottish dialect</i>, which was published on 17 April 1787. Within a week of this event, Burns had sold his copyright to Creech for 100 guineas.<sup id="cite_ref-bold20_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bold20-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> For the edition, Creech commissioned <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Nasmyth" title="Alexander Nasmyth">Alexander Nasmyth</a> to paint the oval bust-length portrait now in the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Portrait_Gallery" title="Scottish National Portrait Gallery">Scottish National Portrait Gallery</a>, which was engraved to provide a frontispiece for the book. Nasmyth had come to know Burns and his fresh and appealing image has become the basis for almost all subsequent representations of the poet.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In Edinburgh, he was received as an equal by the city\'s men of letters\xe2\x80\x94including Dugald Stewart, Robertson, Blair and others\xe2\x80\x94and was a guest at aristocratic gatherings, where he bore himself with unaffected dignity. Here he encountered, and made a lasting impression on, the 16-year-old <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott" title="Walter Scott">Walter Scott</a>, who described him later with great admiration:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>His person was strong and robust; his manners rustic, not clownish, a sort of dignified plainness and simplicity which received part of its effect perhaps from knowledge of his extraordinary talents. His features are presented in Mr Nasmyth\'s picture but to me it conveys the idea that they are diminished, as if seen in perspective. I think his countenance was more massive than it looks in any of the portraits&#160;... there was a strong expression of shrewdness in all his lineaments; the eye alone, I think, indicated the poetical character and temperament. It was large, and of a dark cast, and literally glowed when he spoke with feeling or interest. I never saw such another eye in a human head, though I have seen the most distinguished men of my time.</p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Walter Scott<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Burns_statue,_Bernard_Street.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Robert_Burns_statue%2C_Bernard_Street.jpg/170px-Robert_Burns_statue%2C_Bernard_Street.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Robert_Burns_statue%2C_Bernard_Street.jpg/255px-Robert_Burns_statue%2C_Bernard_Street.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Robert_Burns_statue%2C_Bernard_Street.jpg/340px-Robert_Burns_statue%2C_Bernard_Street.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Burns_statue,_Bernard_Street.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burns statue by David Watson Stevenson (1898) in <a href="/wiki/Leith" title="Leith">Bernard Street, Leith</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The new edition of his poems brought Burns \xc2\xa3400. His stay in the city also resulted in some lifelong friendships, among which were those with <a href="/wiki/James_Cunningham,_13th_Earl_of_Glencairn" class="mw-redirect" title="James Cunningham, 13th Earl of Glencairn">Lord Glencairn</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Frances_Anna_Dunlop" class="mw-redirect" title="Frances Anna Dunlop">Frances Anna Dunlop</a> (1730\xe2\x80\x931815), who became his occasional sponsor and with whom he corresponded for many years until a rift developed. He embarked on a relationship with the separated <a href="/wiki/Agnes_Maclehose" title="Agnes Maclehose">Agnes "Nancy" McLehose</a> (1758\xe2\x80\x931841), with whom he exchanged passionate letters under pseudonyms (Burns called himself "Sylvander" and Nancy "Clarinda"). When it became clear that Nancy would not be easily seduced into a physical relationship, Burns moved on to <a href="/wiki/Jenny_Clow" title="Jenny Clow">Jenny Clow</a> (1766\xe2\x80\x931792), Nancy\'s domestic servant, who bore him a son, Robert Burns Clow, in 1788. He also had an affair with a servant girl, <a href="/wiki/May_Cameron" title="May Cameron">Margaret "May" Cameron</a>. His relationship with Nancy concluded in 1791 with a final meeting in Edinburgh before she sailed to Jamaica for what turned out to be a short-lived reconciliation with her estranged husband. Before she left, he sent her the manuscript of "<a href="/wiki/Ae_Fond_Kiss" title="Ae Fond Kiss">Ae Fond Kiss</a>" as a farewell.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>\nIn Edinburgh, in early 1787, he met James Johnson, a struggling music engraver and music seller with a love of old Scots songs and a determination to preserve them. Burns shared this interest and became an enthusiastic contributor to <i>The Scots Musical Museum</i>. The first volume was published in 1787 and included three songs by Burns. He contributed 40 songs to volume two, and he ended up responsible for about a third of the 600 songs in the whole collection, as well as making a considerable editorial contribution. The final volume was published in 1803.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></p><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dumfriesshire">Dumfriesshire</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ellisland_Farm">Ellisland Farm</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ellisland_Farm,_Dumfries" class="mw-redirect" title="Ellisland Farm, Dumfries">Ellisland Farm, Dumfries</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:River_Nith_at_Ellisland.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/River_Nith_at_Ellisland.JPG/220px-River_Nith_at_Ellisland.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/River_Nith_at_Ellisland.JPG/330px-River_Nith_at_Ellisland.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/River_Nith_at_Ellisland.JPG/440px-River_Nith_at_Ellisland.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2288" data-file-height="1712" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:River_Nith_at_Ellisland.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The River Nith at Ellisland Farm.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellisland_Farm_and_the_River_Nith.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Ellisland_Farm_and_the_River_Nith.jpg/220px-Ellisland_Farm_and_the_River_Nith.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Ellisland_Farm_and_the_River_Nith.jpg/330px-Ellisland_Farm_and_the_River_Nith.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Ellisland_Farm_and_the_River_Nith.jpg/440px-Ellisland_Farm_and_the_River_Nith.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3152" data-file-height="2016" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellisland_Farm_and_the_River_Nith.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ellisland farm in the time of Robert Burns</div></div></div>\n<p>On his return from Edinburgh in February 1788, he resumed his relationship with Jean Armour and took a lease on Ellisland Farm, Dumfriesshire, settling there in June. He also trained as a gauger or <a href="/wiki/Excise" title="Excise">exciseman</a> in case farming continued to be unsuccessful. He was appointed to duties in Customs and Excise in 1789 and eventually gave up the farm in 1791. Meanwhile, in November 1790, he had written "<a href="/wiki/Tam_o%27_Shanter_(poem)" title="Tam o&#39; Shanter (poem)">Tam O\' Shanter</a>". About this time he was offered and declined an appointment in London on the staff of <i>The Star</i> newspaper,<sup id="cite_ref-poetry-poems-poets_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-poetry-poems-poets-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> and refused to become a candidate for a newly created Chair of Agriculture in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-poetry-poems-poets_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-poetry-poems-poets-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> although influential friends offered to support his claims. He did however accept membership of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Company_of_Archers" title="Royal Company of Archers">Royal Company of Archers</a> in 1792.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lyricist">Lyricist</span></h3>\n<p>After giving up his farm, he removed to Dumfries. It was at this time that, being requested to write lyrics for <i>The Melodies of Scotland</i>, he responded by contributing over 100 songs. He made major contributions to <a href="/wiki/George_Thomson_(musician)" title="George Thomson (musician)">George Thomson</a>\'s <i>A Select Collection of Original Scottish Airs for the Voice</i> as well as to James Johnson\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Scots_Musical_Museum" title="Scots Musical Museum">Scots Musical Museum</a></i>. Arguably his claim to immortality chiefly rests on these volumes, which placed him in the front rank of <a href="/wiki/Lyric_poetry" title="Lyric poetry">lyric poets</a>. As a songwriter he provided his own lyrics, sometimes adapted from traditional words. He put words to Scottish folk melodies and airs which he collected, and composed his own arrangements of the music including modifying tunes or recreating melodies on the basis of fragments. In letters he explained that he preferred simplicity, relating songs to spoken language which should be sung in traditional ways. The original instruments would be <a href="/wiki/Fiddle" title="Fiddle">fiddle</a> and the guitar of the period which was akin to a <a href="/wiki/Cittern" title="Cittern">cittern</a>, but the transcription of songs for piano has resulted in them usually being performed in classical concert or music hall styles.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thomson as a publisher commissioned arrangements of "Scottish, Welsh and Irish Airs" by such eminent composers of the day as <a href="/wiki/Franz_Haydn" class="mw-redirect" title="Franz Haydn">Franz Haydn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Ludwig van Beethoven</a>, with new lyrics. The contributors of lyrics included Burns. While such arrangements had wide popular appeal,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Beethoven\'s music was more advanced and difficult to play than Thomson intended.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Burns described how he had to master singing the tune before he composed the words:\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Burns_House,_Dumfries.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Robert_Burns_House%2C_Dumfries.jpg/220px-Robert_Burns_House%2C_Dumfries.jpg" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Robert_Burns_House%2C_Dumfries.jpg/330px-Robert_Burns_House%2C_Dumfries.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Robert_Burns_House%2C_Dumfries.jpg/440px-Robert_Burns_House%2C_Dumfries.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4135" data-file-height="3024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Burns_House,_Dumfries.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burns House in Dumfries, Scotland</div></div></div>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>My way is: I consider the poetic sentiment, correspondent to my idea of the musical expression, then chuse my theme, begin one stanza, when that is composed\xe2\x80\x94which is generally the most difficult part of the business\xe2\x80\x94I walk out, sit down now and then, look out for objects in nature around me that are in unison or harmony with the cogitations of my fancy and workings of my bosom, humming every now and then the air with the verses I have framed. when I feel my Muse beginning to jade, I retire to the solitary fireside of my study, and there commit my effusions to paper, swinging, at intervals, on the hind-legs of my elbow chair, by way of calling forth my own critical strictures, as my, pen goes.</p><dd>\xe2\x80\x94Robert Burns</dd>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Burns also worked to collect and preserve Scottish <a href="/wiki/Folk_songs" class="mw-redirect" title="Folk songs">folk songs</a>, sometimes revising, expanding, and adapting them. One of the better known of these collections is <i>The Merry Muses of Caledonia</i> (the title is not Burns\'s), a collection of bawdy lyrics that were popular in the <a href="/wiki/Music_hall" title="Music hall">music halls</a> of Scotland as late as the 20th century. Many of Burns\'s most famous poems are songs with the music based upon older traditional songs. For example, "<a href="/wiki/Auld_Lang_Syne" title="Auld Lang Syne">Auld Lang Syne</a>" is set to the traditional tune "Can Ye Labour Lea", "<a href="/wiki/A_Red,_Red_Rose" title="A Red, Red Rose">A Red, Red Rose</a>" is set to the tune of "Major Graham" and "<a href="/wiki/The_Battle_of_Sherramuir" title="The Battle of Sherramuir">The Battle of Sherramuir</a>" is set to the "Cameronian Rant".\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Failing_health_and_death">Failing health and death</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Death_room_robert_burns.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Death_room_robert_burns.jpg/220px-Death_room_robert_burns.jpg" width="220" height="129" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Death_room_robert_burns.jpg/330px-Death_room_robert_burns.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Death_room_robert_burns.jpg/440px-Death_room_robert_burns.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3120" data-file-height="1824" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Death_room_robert_burns.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The death room of Robert Burns</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Burns_Mausoleum,_Dumfries.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Robert_Burns_Mausoleum%2C_Dumfries.jpg/170px-Robert_Burns_Mausoleum%2C_Dumfries.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Robert_Burns_Mausoleum%2C_Dumfries.jpg/255px-Robert_Burns_Mausoleum%2C_Dumfries.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Robert_Burns_Mausoleum%2C_Dumfries.jpg/340px-Robert_Burns_Mausoleum%2C_Dumfries.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2318" data-file-height="3476" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Burns_Mausoleum,_Dumfries.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Robert Burns Mausoleum at St. Michael\'s churchyard in Dumfries</div></div></div>\n<p>Burns\'s worldly prospects were perhaps better than they had ever been; but he had become soured, and had alienated many of his best friends by too freely expressing sympathy with the French Revolution and the then unpopular advocates of reform at home. His political views also came to the notice of his employers and in an attempt to prove his loyalty to the Crown, Burns joined the Royal Dumfries <a href="/wiki/British_Volunteer_Corps" title="British Volunteer Corps">Volunteers</a> in March 1795.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> As his health began to give way, he began to age prematurely and fell into fits of despondency.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The habits of intemperance (alleged mainly by temperance activist James Currie)<sup id="cite_ref-R-B-Gallery_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R-B-Gallery-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> are said to have aggravated his long-standing possible rheumatic heart condition.<sup id="cite_ref-Hogg,_Patrick_Scott_2008_p._321_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hogg,_Patrick_Scott_2008_p._321-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On the morning of 21 July 1796, Burns died in Dumfries, at the age of 37. The funeral took place on Monday 25 July 1796, the day that his son Maxwell was born. He was at first buried in the far corner of St. Michael\'s Churchyard in Dumfries; a simple "slab of freestone" was erected as his gravestone by Jean Armour, which some felt insulting to his memory.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> His body was eventually moved to its final location in the same cemetery, the Burns Mausoleum, in September 1817.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The body of his widow Jean Armour was buried with his in 1834.<sup id="cite_ref-Hogg,_Patrick_Scott_2008_p._321_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hogg,_Patrick_Scott_2008_p._321-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Armour had taken steps to secure his personal property, partly by liquidating two promissory notes amounting to fifteen pounds sterling (about 1,100 pounds at 2009 prices).<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The family went to the Court of Session in 1798 with a plan to support his surviving children by publishing a four-volume edition of his complete works and a biography written by Dr. James Currie. Subscriptions were raised to meet the initial cost of publication, which was in the hands of Thomas Cadell and William Davies in London and William Creech, bookseller in Edinburgh.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Hogg records that fund-raising for Burns\'s family was embarrassingly slow, and it took several years to accumulate significant funds through the efforts of John Syme and Alexander Cunningham.<sup id="cite_ref-Hogg,_Patrick_Scott_2008_p._321_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hogg,_Patrick_Scott_2008_p._321-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Burns was posthumously given the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_City" title="Freedom of the City">freedom of the town</a> of Dumfries.<sup id="cite_ref-R-B-Gallery_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R-B-Gallery-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Hogg records that Burns was given the freedom of the Burgh of Dumfries on 4 June 1787, 9 years before his death, and was also made an Honorary Burgess of Dumfries.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Through his twelve children, Burns has over 600 living descendants as of 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_style">Literary style</span></h2>\n<p>Burns\'s style is marked by spontaneity, directness, and sincerity, and ranges from the tender intensity of some of his lyrics through the humour of "Tam o\' Shanter" and the satire of "Holy Willie\'s Prayer" and "The Holy Fair".\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burns_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Burns_2.jpg/170px-Burns_2.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Burns_2.jpg/255px-Burns_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Burns_2.jpg/340px-Burns_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burns_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of Burns in Dumfries town centre, unveiled in 1882</div></div></div>\n<p>Burns\'s poetry drew upon a substantial familiarity with and knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">Classical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Biblical</a>, and <a href="/wiki/English_literature" title="English literature">English literature</a>, as well as the Scottish <a href="/wiki/Makar" title="Makar">Makar</a> tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-Literary-Style_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Literary-Style-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Burns was skilled in writing not only in the <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots language</a> but also in the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_English" title="Scottish English">Scottish English</a> dialect of the English language. Some of his works, such as "Love and Liberty" (also known as "The Jolly Beggars"), are written in both Scots and English for various effects.<sup id="cite_ref-some-hae-meat_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-some-hae-meat-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>His themes included <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a> (he lived during the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolutionary period</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Radicalism_(historical)" title="Radicalism (historical)">Radicalism</a>, which he expressed covertly in "<a href="/wiki/Scots_Wha_Hae" title="Scots Wha Hae">Scots Wha Hae</a>", <a href="/wiki/Scottish_patriotism" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish patriotism">Scottish patriotism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-clericalism" title="Anti-clericalism">anticlericalism</a>, class inequalities, gender roles, commentary on the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Kirk" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Kirk">Scottish Kirk</a> of his time, Scottish cultural identity, poverty, sexuality, and the beneficial aspects of popular socialising (carousing, Scotch whisky, folk songs, and so forth).<sup id="cite_ref-Address-to-the-Kibble_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Address-to-the-Kibble-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The strong emotional highs and lows associated with many of Burns\'s poems have led some, such as Burns biographer Robert Crawford,<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> to suggest that he suffered from <a href="/wiki/Bipolar_disorder" title="Bipolar disorder">manic depression</a>\xe2\x80\x94a hypothesis that has been supported by analysis of various samples of his handwriting. Burns himself referred to suffering from episodes of what he called "blue devilism". The <a href="/wiki/National_Trust_for_Scotland" title="National Trust for Scotland">National Trust for Scotland</a> has downplayed the suggestion on the grounds that evidence is insufficient to support the claim.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Britain">Britain</span></h3>\n<p>Burns is generally classified as a proto-<a href="/wiki/Romantic_Poetry" class="mw-redirect" title="Romantic Poetry">Romantic poet</a>, and he influenced <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">Samuel Taylor Coleridge</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Percy_Bysshe_Shelley" title="Percy Bysshe Shelley">Percy Bysshe Shelley</a> greatly. His direct literary influences in the use of Scots in poetry were <a href="/wiki/Allan_Ramsay_(poet)" title="Allan Ramsay (poet)">Allan Ramsay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fergusson" title="Robert Fergusson">Robert Fergusson</a>. The Edinburgh literati worked to sentimentalise Burns during his life and after his death, dismissing his education by calling him a "heaven-taught ploughman". Burns influenced later Scottish writers, especially <a href="/wiki/Hugh_MacDiarmid" title="Hugh MacDiarmid">Hugh MacDiarmid</a>, who fought to dismantle what he felt had become a sentimental cult that dominated Scottish literature.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:J13379_ArtVille_20110621-160722_RobertBurns_SquareDorchesterMontreal.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/J13379_ArtVille_20110621-160722_RobertBurns_SquareDorchesterMontreal.jpg/170px-J13379_ArtVille_20110621-160722_RobertBurns_SquareDorchesterMontreal.jpg" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/J13379_ArtVille_20110621-160722_RobertBurns_SquareDorchesterMontreal.jpg/255px-J13379_ArtVille_20110621-160722_RobertBurns_SquareDorchesterMontreal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/J13379_ArtVille_20110621-160722_RobertBurns_SquareDorchesterMontreal.jpg/340px-J13379_ArtVille_20110621-160722_RobertBurns_SquareDorchesterMontreal.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="2500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:J13379_ArtVille_20110621-160722_RobertBurns_SquareDorchesterMontreal.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burns Monument in <a href="/wiki/Dorchester_Square" title="Dorchester Square">Dorchester Square</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montr%C3%A9al,_Qu%C3%A9bec" class="mw-redirect" title="Montr\xc3\xa9al, Qu\xc3\xa9bec">Montr\xc3\xa9al, Qu\xc3\xa9bec</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Burns had a significant influence on <a href="/wiki/Alexander_McLachlan_(poet)" title="Alexander McLachlan (poet)">Alexander McLachlan</a><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and some influence on <a href="/wiki/Robert_W._Service" title="Robert W. Service">Robert Service</a>. While this may not be so obvious in Service\'s English verse, which is Kiplingesque, it is more readily apparent in his Scots verse.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scottish Canadians have embraced Robert Burns as a kind of patron poet and mark his birthday with festivities. \'Robbie Burns Day\' is celebrated from <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland and Labrador</a><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> to <a href="/wiki/Nanaimo" title="Nanaimo">Nanaimo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Every year, Canadian newspapers publish biographies of the poet,<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> listings of local events<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> and buffet menus.<sup id="cite_ref-Canadian_Living_January_2013_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canadian_Living_January_2013-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Universities mark the date in a range of ways: <a href="/wiki/McMaster_University" title="McMaster University">McMaster University</a> library organized a special collection<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Simon_Fraser_University" title="Simon Fraser University">Simon Fraser University</a>\'s Centre for Scottish Studies organized a marathon reading of Burns\'s poetry.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Heath_MacQuarrie" title="Heath MacQuarrie">Senator Heath Macquarrie</a> quipped of Canada\'s first Prime Minister that "While the lovable [Robbie] Burns went in for wine, women and song, his fellow Scot, <a href="/wiki/John_A._McDonald" class="mw-redirect" title="John A. McDonald">John A.</a> did not chase women and was not musical!"<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> \'Gung Haggis Fat Choy\' is a hybrid of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_New_Year" title="Chinese New Year">Chinese New Year</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burns_Supper" class="mw-redirect" title="Burns Supper">Robbie Burns Day</a>, celebrated in <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a> since the late 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burns_Commons.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Burns_Commons.jpg/220px-Burns_Commons.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Burns_Commons.jpg/330px-Burns_Commons.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Burns_Commons.jpg/440px-Burns_Commons.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2996" data-file-height="2244" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burns_Commons.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Burns_Commons" title="Burns Commons">Burns Commons</a> in <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee" title="Milwaukee">Milwaukee</a>, USA</div></div></div>\n<p>In January 1864, President <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">Abraham Lincoln</a> was invited to attend a Robert Burns celebration by Robert Crawford; and if unable to attend, send a toast.  Lincoln composed a toast.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>An example of Burns\'s literary influence in the US is seen in the choice by novelist <a href="/wiki/John_Steinbeck" title="John Steinbeck">John Steinbeck</a> of the title of his 1937 novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Of_Mice_and_Men" title="Of Mice and Men">Of Mice and Men</a></i>, taken from a line in the second-to-last stanza of "<a href="/wiki/To_a_Mouse" title="To a Mouse">To a Mouse</a>": "The best laid schemes o\' mice an\' men&#160;/ Gang aft agley." Burns\'s influence on American vernacular poets such as <a href="/wiki/James_Whitcomb_Riley#See_also" title="James Whitcomb Riley">James Whitcomb Riley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Lebby_Stanton" title="Frank Lebby Stanton">Frank Lebby Stanton</a> has been acknowledged by their biographers.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> When asked for the source of his greatest creative inspiration, singer songwriter <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan" title="Bob Dylan">Bob Dylan</a> selected Burns\'s 1794 song "<a href="/wiki/A_Red,_Red_Rose" title="A Red, Red Rose">A Red, Red Rose</a>" as the lyric that had the biggest effect on his life.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>The author <a href="/wiki/J._D._Salinger" title="J. D. Salinger">J. D. Salinger</a> used protagonist Holden Caulfield\'s misinterpretation of Burns\'s poem "<a href="/wiki/Comin%27_Through_the_Rye" class="mw-redirect" title="Comin&#39; Through the Rye">Comin\' Through the Rye</a>" as his title and a main interpretation of Caulfield\'s grasping to his childhood in his 1951 novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Catcher_in_the_Rye" title="The Catcher in the Rye">The Catcher in the Rye</a></i>. The poem, actually about a rendezvous, is thought by Caulfield to be about saving people from falling out of childhood.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span></h3>\n<p>Burns became the "people\'s poet" of Russia. In <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Russia" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Russia">Imperial Russia</a> Burns was translated into Russian and became a source of inspiration for the ordinary, oppressed Russian people. In <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet</a> Russia, he was elevated as the archetypal poet of the people. As a great admirer of the egalitarian ethos behind the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolutions</a> who expressed his own egalitarianism in poems such as his "Birthday Ode for George Washington" or his "<a href="/wiki/Is_There_for_Honest_Poverty" title="Is There for Honest Poverty">Is There for Honest Poverty</a>" (commonly known as "A Man\'s a Man for a\' that"), Burns was well placed for endorsement by the <a href="/wiki/Communist" class="mw-redirect" title="Communist">Communist</a> regime as a "progressive" artist. A new translation of Burns begun in 1924 by <a href="/wiki/Samuil_Marshak" title="Samuil Marshak">Samuil Marshak</a> proved enormously popular, selling over 600,000 copies.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The USSR honoured Burns with a commemorative stamp in 1956. He remains popular in Russia after the fall of the Soviet Union.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Honours">Honours</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Landmarks_and_organisations">Landmarks and organisations</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellisland_Farm,_Dumfries,_c_1900.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Ellisland_Farm%2C_Dumfries%2C_c_1900.jpg/220px-Ellisland_Farm%2C_Dumfries%2C_c_1900.jpg" width="220" height="121" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Ellisland_Farm%2C_Dumfries%2C_c_1900.jpg/330px-Ellisland_Farm%2C_Dumfries%2C_c_1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Ellisland_Farm%2C_Dumfries%2C_c_1900.jpg/440px-Ellisland_Farm%2C_Dumfries%2C_c_1900.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="1792" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellisland_Farm,_Dumfries,_c_1900.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ellisland_Farm,_Dumfries" class="mw-redirect" title="Ellisland Farm, Dumfries">Ellisland Farm</a> c.&#160;1900</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Confederate_Park,_Jacksonville,_FL,_US.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Confederate_Park%2C_Jacksonville%2C_FL%2C_US.jpg/220px-Confederate_Park%2C_Jacksonville%2C_FL%2C_US.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Confederate_Park%2C_Jacksonville%2C_FL%2C_US.jpg/330px-Confederate_Park%2C_Jacksonville%2C_FL%2C_US.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Confederate_Park%2C_Jacksonville%2C_FL%2C_US.jpg/440px-Confederate_Park%2C_Jacksonville%2C_FL%2C_US.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4016" data-file-height="6016" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Confederate_Park,_Jacksonville,_FL,_US.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue in <a href="/wiki/Confederate_Park_(Jacksonville)" title="Confederate Park (Jacksonville)">Confederate Park</a>, by the Robert Burns Association of <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Jacksonville, Florida">Jacksonville, Florida</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Burns_Clubs" title="Burns Clubs">Burns clubs</a> have been founded worldwide. The first one, known as The Mother Club, was founded in <a href="/wiki/Greenock" title="Greenock">Greenock</a> in 1801 by merchants born in <a href="/wiki/Ayrshire" title="Ayrshire">Ayrshire</a>, some of whom had known Burns.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The club set its original objectives as "To cherish the name of Robert Burns; to foster a love of his writings, and generally to encourage an interest in the Scottish language and literature." The club also continues to have local charitable work as a priority.<sup id="cite_ref-mother_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mother-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Burns\'s birthplace in Alloway is now a public museum known as <a href="/wiki/Burns_Cottage" title="Burns Cottage">Burns Cottage</a>. His house in Dumfries is operated as the Robert Burns House, and the Robert Burns Centre in Dumfries features more exhibits about his life and works. <a href="/wiki/Ellisland_Farm,_Dumfries" class="mw-redirect" title="Ellisland Farm, Dumfries">Ellisland Farm</a> in <a href="/wiki/Auldgirth" title="Auldgirth">Auldgirth</a>, which he owned from 1788 to 1791, is maintained as a working farm with a museum and interpretation centre by the Friends of Ellisland Farm.\n</p><p>Significant 19th-century monuments to him stand in Alloway, Leith, and Dumfries. An early 20th-century replica of his birthplace cottage belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Burns_Club_Atlanta" title="Burns Club Atlanta">Burns Club Atlanta</a> stands in <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>. These are part of a large <a href="/wiki/List_of_Robert_Burns_memorials" title="List of Robert Burns memorials">list of Burns memorials and statues</a> around the world.\n</p><p>Organisations include the <a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns_Fellowship" title="Robert Burns Fellowship">Robert Burns Fellowship</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Otago" title="University of Otago">University of Otago</a> in New Zealand, and the <a href="/wiki/Burns_Club_Atlanta" title="Burns Club Atlanta">Burns Club Atlanta</a> in the United States. Towns named after Burns include <a href="/wiki/Burns,_New_York" title="Burns, New York">Burns, New York</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Burns,_Oregon" title="Burns, Oregon">Burns, Oregon</a>.\n</p><p>In the suburb of <a href="/wiki/Summerhill,_Dumfries" title="Summerhill, Dumfries">Summerhill, Dumfries</a>, the majority of the streets have names with Burns connotations. A <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Standard_Class_7" class="mw-redirect" title="British Rail Standard Class 7">British Rail Standard Class 7</a> steam locomotive was named after him, along with a later <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_87" title="British Rail Class 87">Class 87</a> electric locomotive, No. 87035. On 24 September 1996, <a href="/wiki/British_Rail_Class_156" title="British Rail Class 156">Class 156</a> diesel unit 156433 was named "The Kilmarnock Edition" by Jimmy Knapp, General Secretary of the <a href="/wiki/National_Union_of_Rail,_Maritime_and_Transport_Workers" title="National Union of Rail, Maritime and Transport Workers">RMT</a> union, at Girvan Station to launch the new "Burns Line" services between Girvan, Ayr, and Kilmarnock, supported by <a href="/wiki/Strathclyde_Partnership_for_Transport" title="Strathclyde Partnership for Transport">Strathclyde Passenger Transport (SPT)</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burns_Landmarks_and_organisations.jpg" class="image"><img alt="statue of man on a tall base in a park" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Burns_Landmarks_and_organisations.jpg/220px-Burns_Landmarks_and_organisations.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Burns_Landmarks_and_organisations.jpg/330px-Burns_Landmarks_and_organisations.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Burns_Landmarks_and_organisations.jpg/440px-Burns_Landmarks_and_organisations.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1300" data-file-height="1300" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burns_Landmarks_and_organisations.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burns statue in <a href="/wiki/Treasury_Gardens" title="Treasury Gardens">Treasury Gardens</a>, Melbourne, Victoria, Australia</div></div></div>\n<p>Several streets surrounding the <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Law_Olmsted,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick Law Olmsted, Jr.">Frederick Law Olmsted, Jr.</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Back_Bay_Fens" class="mw-redirect" title="Back Bay Fens">Back Bay Fens</a> in <a href="/wiki/Boston,_Massachusetts" class="mw-redirect" title="Boston, Massachusetts">Boston, Massachusetts</a>, were designated with Burns connotations. A life-size statue was dedicated in Burns\'s honour within the Back Bay Fens of the West Fenway neighbourhood in 1912. It stood until 1972 when it was relocated downtown, sparking protests from the neighbourhood, literary fans, and preservationists of Olmsted\'s vision for the Back Bay Fens.\n</p><p>There is a statue of Burns in <a href="/wiki/The_Octagon,_Dunedin" title="The Octagon, Dunedin">The Octagon, Dunedin</a>, in the same pose as the one in Dundee. Dunedin\'s first European settlers were Scots; Thomas Burns, a nephew of Burns, was one of Dunedin\'s founding fathers.\n</p><p>A crater on Mercury is named after Burns.\n</p><p>In November 2012, Burns was awarded the title Honorary Chartered Surveyor<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> by The Royal Institution of Chartered Surveyors, the only posthumous membership so far granted by the institution.\n</p><p>The oldest statue of Burns is in the town of <a href="/wiki/Camperdown,_Victoria" title="Camperdown, Victoria">Camperdown, Victoria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> It now hosts an annual Robert Burns Scottish Festival in celebration of the statue and its history.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stamps_and_currency">Stamps and currency</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ussr-robertburns1956.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ussr-robertburns1956.jpg/170px-Ussr-robertburns1956.jpg" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ussr-robertburns1956.jpg/255px-Ussr-robertburns1956.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Ussr-robertburns1956.jpg/340px-Ussr-robertburns1956.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1461" data-file-height="2020" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ussr-robertburns1956.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Burns stamp, <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">USSR</a> 1956</div></div></div>\n<p>The Soviet Union was the first country in the world to honour Burns with a commemorative stamp, marking the 160th anniversary of his death in 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail" title="Royal Mail">Royal Mail</a> has issued postage stamps commemorating Burns three times. In 1966, two stamps were issued, priced fourpence and one shilling and threepence, both carrying Burns\'s portrait. In 1996, an issue commemorating the bicentenary of his death comprised four stamps, priced 19p, 25p, 41p and 60p and including quotes from Burns\'s poems. On 22 January 2009, two stamps were issued by the Royal Mail to commemorate the 250th anniversary of Burns\'s birth.\n</p><p>Burns was pictured on the <a href="/wiki/Clydesdale_Bank#Banknotes" title="Clydesdale Bank">Clydesdale Bank</a> \xc2\xa35 note from 1971 to 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-scotbanks1_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scotbanks1-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clydesdalenew_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clydesdalenew-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> On the reverse of the note was a vignette of a <a href="/wiki/Apodemus" title="Apodemus">field mouse</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Rosa_acicularis" title="Rosa acicularis">wild rose</a> in reference to Burns\'s poem "To a Mouse". The Clydesdale Bank\'s notes were redesigned in 2009 and, since then, he has been pictured on the front of their \xc2\xa310 note.<sup id="cite_ref-Clydesdalenew_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clydesdalenew-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In September 2007, the Bank of Scotland redesigned their banknotes to feature famous Scottish bridges. The reverse side of new \xc2\xa35 features Brig o\' Doon, famous from Burns\'s poem "Tam o\' Shanter", and pictures the statue of Burns at that site.<sup id="cite_ref-scotbanks2_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scotbanks2-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1996, the Isle of Man issued a four-coin set of Crown (5/-) pieces on the themes of "Auld Lang Syne", Edinburgh Castle, Revenue Cutter, and Writing Poems.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Tristan da Cunha produced a gold \xc2\xa35 Bicentenary Coin.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2009 the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mint" title="Royal Mint">Royal Mint</a> issued a commemorative <a href="/wiki/Two_pound_coin" class="mw-redirect" title="Two pound coin">two pound coin</a> featuring a quote from "Auld Lang Syne".<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_tributes">Musical tributes</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Burns_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Robert_Burns_1.jpg/170px-Robert_Burns_1.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Robert_Burns_1.jpg/255px-Robert_Burns_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Robert_Burns_1.jpg/340px-Robert_Burns_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="798" data-file-height="1067" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Robert_Burns_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Engraved version of the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Nasmyth" title="Alexander Nasmyth">Alexander Nasmyth</a> 1787 portrait</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1976, singer <a href="/wiki/Jean_Redpath" title="Jean Redpath">Jean Redpath</a>, in collaboration with composer <a href="/wiki/Serge_Hovey" title="Serge Hovey">Serge Hovey</a>, started to record all of Burns\'s songs, with a mixture of traditional and Burns\'s own compositions. The project ended when Hovey died, after seven of the planned twenty-two volumes were completed. Redpath also recorded four cassettes of Burns\'s songs (re-issued as 3 CDs) for the <a href="/wiki/Scots_Musical_Museum" title="Scots Musical Museum">Scots Musical Museum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1996, a musical about Burns\'s life called <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Red_Red_Rose&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Red Red Rose (page does not exist)">Red Red Rose</a></i> won third place at a competition for new musicals in Denmark. Robert Burns was played by <a href="/wiki/John_Barrowman" title="John Barrowman">John Barrowman</a>. On 25 January 2008, a musical play about the love affair between Robert Burns and Nancy McLehose entitled <i>Clarinda</i> premiered in Edinburgh before touring Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The plan was that <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Clarinda_(musical)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Clarinda (musical) (page does not exist)">Clarinda</a></i> would make its American premiere in Atlantic Beach, FL, at Atlantic Beach Experimental Theatre on 25 January 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eddi_Reader" title="Eddi Reader">Eddi Reader</a> has released two albums, <i><a href="/wiki/Sings_the_Songs_of_Robert_Burns" title="Sings the Songs of Robert Burns">Sings the Songs of Robert Burns</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Songs_of_Robert_Burns_Deluxe_Edition#Deluxe_edition" class="mw-redirect" title="The Songs of Robert Burns Deluxe Edition">The Songs of Robert Burns Deluxe Edition</a></i>, about the work of the poet.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alfred_Billings_Street" title="Alfred Billings Street">Alfred B. Street</a> wrote the words and Henry Tucker wrote the music for a song called <i>Our Own Robbie Burns</i><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> in 1856.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burns_suppers">Burns suppers</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Burns_supper" title="Burns supper">Burns supper</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cutting_the_haggis.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Cutting_the_haggis.jpg/220px-Cutting_the_haggis.jpg" width="220" height="113" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Cutting_the_haggis.jpg/330px-Cutting_the_haggis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Cutting_the_haggis.jpg/440px-Cutting_the_haggis.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2265" data-file-height="1161" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cutting_the_haggis.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Great chieftain o\' the puddin-race!" \xe2\x80\x93 cutting the haggis at a <a href="/wiki/Burns_supper" title="Burns supper">Burns supper</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Burns Night, in effect a second <a href="/wiki/National_day" title="National day">national day</a>, is celebrated on Burns\'s birthday, 25 January, with <a href="/wiki/Burns_supper" title="Burns supper">Burns suppers</a> around the world, and is more widely observed in Scotland than the official national day, <a href="/wiki/St._Andrew%27s_Day" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Andrew&#39;s Day">St. Andrew\'s Day</a>. The first Burns supper in The <a href="/wiki/Burns_Clubs" title="Burns Clubs">Mother Club</a> in Greenock was held on what was thought to be his birthday on 29 January 1802; in 1803 it was discovered from the Ayr parish records that the correct date was 25 January 1759.<sup id="cite_ref-mother_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mother-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The format of Burns suppers has changed little since. The basic format starts with a general welcome and announcements, followed with <a href="/wiki/Selkirk,_Scottish_Borders#The_Selkirk_Grace" title="Selkirk, Scottish Borders">the Selkirk Grace</a>. After the grace comes the piping and cutting of the <a href="/wiki/Haggis" title="Haggis">haggis</a>, when Burns\'s famous "<a href="/wiki/Address_to_a_Haggis" class="mw-redirect" title="Address to a Haggis">Address to a Haggis</a>" is read and the haggis is cut open. The event usually allows for people to start eating just after the haggis is presented. At the end of the meal, a series of toasts, often including a \'Toast to the Lassies\', and replies are made. This is when the toast to "the immortal memory", an overview of Burns\'s life and work, is given. The event usually concludes with the singing of "Auld Lang Syne".\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greatest_Scot">Greatest Scot</span></h3>\n<p>In 2009, <a href="/wiki/STV_(TV_network)" class="mw-redirect" title="STV (TV network)">STV</a> ran a television series and public vote on who was "The Greatest Scot" of all time.  Robert Burns won, narrowly beating <a href="/wiki/William_Wallace" title="William Wallace">William Wallace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> A bust of Burns is in the Hall of Heroes of the <a href="/wiki/National_Wallace_Monument" class="mw-redirect" title="National Wallace Monument">National Wallace Monument</a> in <a href="/wiki/Stirling" title="Stirling">Stirling</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<div role="navigation" aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" London: <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Mail" title="Daily Mail">Daily Mail</a></i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">13 July</span> 2009</span>. <q>Bob Dylan has named his own greatest inspiration as the Scottish poet Robert Burns. The American singer-songwriter was asked to say which lyric or verse has had the biggest effect on his life.
